	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Humanism</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about generic "human-centred philosophy". For Renaissance humanism, see <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Humanist" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Humanist_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Humanist (disambiguation)">Humanist (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Humanistic" redirects here. For the album, see <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_(album)" title="Humanistic (album)">Humanistic (album)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">philosophical and ethical stance that emphasizes the value and agency of human beings</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Hebrew" title="Help:Hebrew">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of Hebrew letters.</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Humanism</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> stance that emphasizes the value and <a href="/wiki/Agency_(philosophy)" title="Agency (philosophy)">agency</a> of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human beings</a>, individually and collectively, and generally prefers <a href="/wiki/Critical_thinking" title="Critical thinking">critical thinking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>) over acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>. The meaning of the term <i>humanism</i> has fluctuated according to the successive intellectual movements which have identified with it.<sup id="cite_ref-What_2004_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_2004-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The term was coined by theologian <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Immanuel_Niethammer" title="Friedrich Immanuel Niethammer">Friedrich Niethammer</a> at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a> to refer to a system of education based on the study of classical literature ("classical humanism"). Generally, however, humanism refers to a perspective that affirms some notion of human <a href="/wiki/Freedom" title="Freedom">freedom</a> and progress. It views humans as solely responsible for the promotion and development of individuals and emphasizes a concern for man in relation to the world.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, humanist movements are typically <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">non-religious movements</a> aligned with <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>, and today humanism may refer to a <a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">nontheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a> centred on human agency and looking to <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> source to understand the world.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<p>The word "humanism" is ultimately derived from the Latin concept <i><a href="/wiki/Humanitas" title="Humanitas">humanitas</a></i>. It entered English in the nineteenth century. However, historians agree that the concept predates the label invented to describe it, encompassing the various meanings ascribed to <i>humanitas</i>, which included both benevolence toward one's fellow humans and the values imparted by <i>bonae litterae</i> or humane learning (literally "good letters").
</p><p>In the second century AD, a Latin grammarian, <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Gellius" title="Aulus Gellius">Aulus Gellius</a> (c.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>125&#160;&#8211;&#160;c.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>180), complained:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Those who have spoken Latin and have used the language correctly do not give to the word <i>humanitas</i> the meaning which it is commonly thought to have, namely, what the Greeks call φιλανθρωπία (<a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>), signifying a kind of friendly spirit and good-feeling towards all men without distinction; but they gave to <i>humanitas</i> the force of the Greek παιδεία (<a href="/wiki/Paideia" title="Paideia">paideia</a>); that is, what we call <i>eruditionem institutionemque in bonas artes</i>, or "education and training in the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a>". Those who earnestly desire and seek after these are most highly humanized. For the desire to pursue of that kind of knowledge, and the training given by it, has been granted to humanity alone of all the animals, and for that reason it is termed <i>humanitas</i>, or "humanity".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Gellius says that in his day <i>humanitas</i> is commonly used as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;or kindness and benevolence toward one's fellow human beings. Gellius maintains that this common usage is wrong, and that model writers of Latin, such as Cicero and others, used the word only to mean what we might call "humane" or "polite" learning, or the Greek equivalent <a href="/wiki/Paideia" title="Paideia">Paideia</a>. Yet in seeking to restrict the meaning of <i>humanitas</i> to literary education this way, Gellius was not advocating a retreat from political engagement into some ivory tower, though it might look like that to us. He himself was involved in public affairs. According to legal historian Richard Bauman, Gellius was a judge as well as a grammarian and was an active participant the great contemporary debate on harsh punishments that accompanied the legal reforms of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> (one these reforms,  for example, was that a prisoner was not to be treated as guilty before being tried). "By assigning pride of place to Paideia in his comment on the etymology of <i>humanitas</i>, Gellius implies that the trained mind is best equipped to handle the problems troubling society."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gellius's writings fell into obscurity during the Middle Ages, but during the Italian Renaissance, Gellius became a favorite author. Teachers and scholars of Greek and Latin grammar, rhetoric, philosophy, and poetry were called and called themselves "humanists".<sup id="cite_ref-mann_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oxford-renaissance_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-renaissance-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Modern scholars, however, point out that <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> (106&#160;&#8211;&#160;43<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BCE), who was most responsible for defining and popularizing the term <i>humanitas</i>, in fact frequently used the word in both senses, as did his near contemporaries. For Cicero, a lawyer, what most distinguished humans from brutes was speech, which, allied to reason, could (and should) enable them to settle disputes and live together in concord and harmony under the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Thus <i>humanitas</i> included two meanings from the outset and these continue in the modern derivative, <i>humanism</i>, which even today can refer to both humanitarian benevolence and to a method of study and debate involving an accepted group of authors and a careful and accurate use of language.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, and soon after, in Germany (by the <a href="/wiki/Left_Hegelians" class="mw-redirect" title="Left Hegelians">Left Hegelians</a>), <i>humanism</i> began to refer to an ethical philosophy centered on humankind, without attention to the <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>. The designation <a href="/wiki/Religious_Humanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Humanism">Religious Humanism</a> refers to organized groups that sprang up during the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. It is similar to <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, although centered on human needs, interests, and abilities rather than the supernatural.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In the Anglophone world, such modern, organized forms of humanism, which are rooted in the 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, have to a considerable extent more or less detached themselves from the historic connection of humanism with <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical learning</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts_education" title="Liberal arts education">liberal arts</a>.
</p><p>The first <i><a href="/wiki/Humanist_Manifesto_I" title="Humanist Manifesto I">Humanist Manifesto</a></i> was issued by a conference held at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Signatories included the philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>, but the majority were ministers (chiefly <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Academic_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Academic theology">theologians</a>. They identified humanism as an ideology that espouses <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social and economic justice</a>, and they called for science to replace <a href="/wiki/Dogma#In_religion" title="Dogma">dogma</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> as the basis of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and decision-making.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg/220px-Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="310" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg/330px-Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="372" data-file-height="524" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Isola_di_Utopia_Moro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ideal society as conceived by Renaissance humanist Saint <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Utopia_(book)" title="Utopia (book)">Utopia</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In 1808 Bavarian educational commissioner <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Immanuel_Niethammer" title="Friedrich Immanuel Niethammer">Friedrich Immanuel Niethammer</a> coined the term <i>Humanismus</i> to describe the new <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical curriculum</a> he planned to offer in German secondary schools,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and by 1836 the word "humanism" had been absorbed into the English language in this sense. The coinage gained universal acceptance in 1856, when German historian and philologist <a href="/wiki/Georg_Voigt" title="Georg Voigt">Georg Voigt</a> used <i>humanism</i> to describe <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a>, the movement that flourished in the Italian <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> to revive classical learning, a use which won wide acceptance among historians in many nations, especially Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-See_1877_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See_1877-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>But in the mid-18th century, during the French Enlightenment, a more ideological use of the term had come into use. In 1765, the author of an anonymous article in a French Enlightenment periodical spoke of "The general love of humanity ... a virtue hitherto quite nameless among us, and which we will venture to call 'humanism', for the time has come to create a word for such a beautiful and necessary thing".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The latter part of the 18th and the early 19th centuries saw the creation of numerous grass-roots "philanthropic" and benevolent societies dedicated to human betterment and the spreading of knowledge (some Christian, some not). After the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, the idea that human virtue could be created by human reason alone independently from traditional religious institutions, attributed by opponents of the Revolution to <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">philosophes</a></i> such as <a href="/wiki/Rousseau" class="mw-redirect" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a>, was violently attacked by influential religious and political <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatives</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Maistre" title="Joseph de Maistre">Joseph de Maistre</a>, as a deification or idolatry of humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Humanism began to acquire a negative sense. The <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i> records the use of the word "humanism" by an English clergyman in 1812 to indicate those who believe in the "mere humanity" (as opposed to the divine nature) of Christ, i.e., <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deists" class="mw-redirect" title="Deists">Deists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In this polarised atmosphere, in which established ecclesiastical bodies tended to <a href="/wiki/Circle_the_wagons" class="mw-redirect" title="Circle the wagons">circle the wagons</a> and reflexively oppose political and social reforms like extending the franchise, universal schooling, and the like, liberal reformers and radicals embraced the idea of Humanism as an alternative religion of humanity. The anarchist <a href="/wiki/Proudhon" class="mw-redirect" title="Proudhon">Proudhon</a> (best known for declaring that "<a href="/wiki/Property_is_theft" class="mw-redirect" title="Property is theft">property is theft</a>") used the word "humanism" to describe a "<i>culte, déification de l’humanité</i>" ("worship,  deification of humanity") and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Renan" title="Ernest Renan">Ernest Renan</a> in <i>L’avenir de la science: pensées de 1848</i> ("The Future of Knowledge: Thoughts on 1848") (1848–49), states: "It is my deep conviction that pure <i>humanism</i> will be the religion of the future, that is, the cult of all that pertains to humanity—all of life, sanctified and raised to the level of a moral value."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about the same time, the word "humanism" as a philosophy centred on humankind (as opposed to institutionalised religion) was also being used in Germany by the <a href="/wiki/Left_Hegelians" class="mw-redirect" title="Left Hegelians">Left Hegelians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Ruge" title="Arnold Ruge">Arnold Ruge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, who were critical of the close involvement of the church in the German government. There has been a persistent confusion between the several uses of the terms:<sup id="cite_ref-What_2004_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_2004-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> philanthropic humanists look to what they consider their antecedents in critical thinking and human-centered philosophy among the Greek philosophers and the great figures of Renaissance history; and scholarly humanists stress the linguistic and cultural disciplines needed to understand and interpret these philosophers and artists.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessors">Predecessors</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_India">Ancient India</span></h4>
<p>Human-centered philosophy that rejected the supernatural may also be found circa 1500 BCE in the <a href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" class="mw-redirect" title="Cārvāka">Lokayata</a> system of Indian philosophy. <a href="/wiki/Nasadiya_Sukta" title="Nasadiya Sukta">Nasadiya Sukta</a>, a passage in the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rig Veda</a>, contains one of the first recorded assertions of agnosticism. In the 6th century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a> expressed, in <a href="/wiki/Pali_literature" title="Pali literature">Pali literature</a> a skeptical attitude toward the supernatural:<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Since neither soul, nor aught belonging to soul, can really and truly exist, the view which holds that this I who am 'world', who am 'soul', shall hereafter live permanent, persisting, unchanging, yea abide eternally: is not this utterly and entirely a foolish doctrine?</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_China">Ancient China</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a></div>
<p>The philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (551–479 BCE), which eventually became the basis of the state ideology of successive Chinese dynasties and <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">nearby polities in East Asia</a>, contains several humanistic traits, placing a high value on human life and discounting mysticism and superstition, including speculations on ghosts and an afterlife.  These values are clearly espoused in the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects of Confucius</a></i>, a compilation of quotes and anecdotes attributed to Confucius by his students and philosophical school.
</p><p>In Chapter 10 of the <i>Analects</i>, an incident involving a fire in the stables is recounted:  "The stables burned.  The Master withdrew from court and asked, 'Was anybody hurt?'  He did not inquire about the horses."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  This incident is interpreted to illustrate the priority that Confucius placed on human life over any economic losses associated with the fire.  Later, in Chapter 11, a disciple, Ji Lu, asks Confucius on how to properly serve ghosts and spirits, and what the Master knows about death.  Confucius replied, "If you do not know the proper way to serve people, what need is there to discuss how to serve ghosts?  If you do not understand life, what is the point of understanding death?"<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  In Chapter 15, the <i>Analects</i> gives the passive form of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">Golden Rule</a> ('the Silver Rule').  When asked for a single word to live one's life in accordance with, Confucius gives the reply, leniency (恕), elaborating, "Do not impose upon others that which you yourself would not desire."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Subsequent Confucian philosophers during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (475–221 BCE), including <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xun_Kuang" title="Xun Kuang">Xunzi</a>, likewise centered their philosophies on secular, humanistic concerns, like the nature of good governance and the role of education, rather than ideas founded on the state or folk religions of the time.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek philosophy</a></div>
<p>Sixth-century <a href="/wiki/BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="BCE">BCE</a> <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Socratic">pre-Socratic</a> Greek philosophers <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophanes_of_Colophon" class="mw-redirect" title="Xenophanes of Colophon">Xenophanes of Colophon</a> were the first in the region to attempt to explain the world in terms of human reason rather than myth and tradition, thus can be said to be the first Greek humanists. Thales questioned the notion of anthropomorphic gods and Xenophanes refused to recognise the gods of his time and reserved the divine for the principle of unity in the universe. These Ionian Greeks were the first thinkers to assert that nature is available to be studied separately from the supernatural realm. <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> brought philosophy and the spirit of rational inquiry from Ionia to Athens. <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>, the leader of Athens during the period of its greatest glory was an admirer of Anaxagoras. Other influential pre-Socratics or rational philosophers include <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">Protagoras</a> (like Anaxagoras a friend of Pericles), known for his famous dictum "man is the measure of all things" and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>, who proposed that matter was composed of atoms. Little of the written work of these early philosophers survives and they are known mainly from fragments and quotations in other writers, principally <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. The historian <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>, noted for his scientific and rational approach to history, is also much admired by later humanists.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the 3rd century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> became known for his concise phrasing of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>, lack of belief in the afterlife, and human-centred approaches to achieving <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a>. He was also the first Greek philosopher to admit women to his school as a rule.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Islam">Medieval Islam</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">Early Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<p>Many medieval Muslim thinkers pursued humanistic, <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rational</a> and scientific <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourses</a> in their search for knowledge, meaning and <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" class="mw-redirect" title="Value (personal and cultural)">values</a>. A wide range of Islamic writings on love, poetry, history and <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_theology" title="Philosophical theology">philosophical theology</a> show that medieval Islamic thought was open to the humanistic ideas of <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a>, occasional <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodman_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Imad-ad-Dean Ahmad, another reason the Islamic world flourished during the Middle Ages was an early emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, as summarised by al-Hashimi (a cousin of Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma&#39;mun">al-Ma'mun</a>) in the following letter to one of the religious opponents he was attempting to <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">convert</a> through <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Bring forward all the arguments you wish and say whatever you please and speak your mind freely. Now that you are safe and free to say whatever you please appoint some arbitrator who will impartially judge between us and lean only towards the truth and be free from the empery of passion, and that arbitrator shall be <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">Reason</a>, whereby God makes us responsible for our own rewards and punishments. Herein I have dealt justly with you and have given you full security and am ready to accept whatever decision Reason may give for me or against me. For "There is no compulsion in religion" (<a href="/wiki/Al-Baqara" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Baqara">Qur'an 2</a>:256) and I have only invited you to accept our faith willingly and of your own accord and have pointed out the hideousness of your present belief. Peace be with you and the blessings of God!
</p></blockquote>
<p>According to George Makdisi, certain aspects of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a> has its roots in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">medieval Islamic world</a>, including the "art of <i><a href="/wiki/Dictation_(exercise)" title="Dictation (exercise)">dictation</a></i>, called in Latin, <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_dictaminis" title="Ars dictaminis">ars dictaminis</a></i>", and "the humanist attitude toward <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Icelandic_Sagas">The Icelandic Sagas</span></h4>
<p>Scholars including <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Grimm" title="Jacob Grimm">Jacob Grimm</a>, <a href="/wiki/J.R.R._Tolkien" class="mw-redirect" title="J.R.R. Tolkien">J.R.R. Tolkien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Turville-Petre" title="Gabriel Turville-Petre">E.O.G. Turville-Petre</a> have identified a stream of humanistic philosophy in the <a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">Icelandic sagas</a>.  People described as goðlauss ("without gods") expressed not only a lack of faith in deities, but also a pragmatic belief in their own faculties of strength, reason and virtue and in social codes of honor independent of any supernatural agency.
</p><p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mythologie" title="Deutsche Mythologie">Teutonic Mythology</a></i> (1835), Grimm wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is remarkable that Old Norse legend occasionally mentions certain men who, turning away in utter disgust and doubt from the heathen faith, placed their reliance on their own strength and virtue. Thus in the <i>Solar Lioð 17</i> we read of Vebogi and Radey "a sik þau truðu" – in themselves they trusted; of King Hakon (<i>Fornm. sög. 1, 35</i>) "konungr gerir sem allir aðrir, þeir sem trua a matt sinn ok megin" – the king does like all others who trust in their own might and main; of Barðr (<i>ibid. 2, 151</i>) "ek trui ekki a skurðgoð eðr fiandr, hefi ek þvi lengi truat a matt minn ok megin" – I trust not in idols and fiends; I have held, this long while, faith in my own powers.
</p></blockquote>
<p>In <i>Myth and Religion of the North</i> (1964), Turville-Petre argued that many of the strophes of the Gestaþáttr and Loddfáfnismál sections of the <i><a href="/wiki/Havamal" class="mw-redirect" title="Havamal">Havamal</a></i> express goðlauss sentiments despite being poetically attributed to the god <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a>.  These strophes include numerous items of advice on good conduct and worldly wisdom.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca,_altichiero,_1376_circa,_padova.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg/220px-Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg/330px-Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg/440px-Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca%2C_altichiero%2C_1376_circa%2C_padova.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1348" data-file-height="1276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ritratto_di_francesco_petrarca,_altichiero,_1376_circa,_padova.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> painted in 1376</div></div></div>
<p>Renaissance humanism was an intellectual movement in Europe of the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern">Early Modern</a> period. The 19th-century German historian <a href="/wiki/Georg_Voigt" title="Georg Voigt">Georg Voigt</a> (1827–91) identified <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> as the first Renaissance humanist. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Johnson_(writer)" title="Paul Johnson (writer)">Paul Johnson</a> agrees that Petrarch was "the first to put into words the notion that the centuries between the fall of Rome and the present had been the age of Darkness". According to Petrarch, what was needed to remedy this situation was the careful study and imitation of the great classical authors. For Petrarch and <a href="/wiki/Boccaccio" class="mw-redirect" title="Boccaccio">Boccaccio</a>, the greatest master was <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, whose prose became the model for both learned (Latin) and vernacular (Italian) prose.
</p>
<blockquote><p>Once the language was mastered grammatically it could be used to attain the second stage, eloquence or rhetoric. This art of persuasion [Cicero had held] was not art for its own sake, but the acquisition of the capacity to persuade others – all men and women – to lead the good life. As Petrarch put it, 'it is better to will the good than to know the truth'. Rhetoric thus led to and embraced philosophy. <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> (c.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>1369–1444), the outstanding scholar of the new generation, insisted that it was Petrarch who "opened the way for us to show how to acquire learning", but it was in Bruni's time that the word <i>umanista</i> first came into use, and its subjects of study were listed as five: grammar, rhetoric, poetry, moral philosophy, and history".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salutati.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/220px-Salutati.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="346" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/330px-Salutati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Salutati.jpg/440px-Salutati.jpg 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salutati.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coluccio Salutati, Chancellor of Florence and disciple of Petrarch (1331–1406)</div></div></div>
<p>The basic training of the humanist was to speak well and write (typically, in the form of a letter). One of Petrarch's followers, <a href="/wiki/Coluccio_Salutati" title="Coluccio Salutati">Coluccio Salutati</a> (1331–1406) was made chancellor of <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, "whose interests he defended with his literary skill. The Visconti of Milan claimed that Salutati’s pen had done more damage than 'thirty squadrons of Florentine cavalry'".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/220px-Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/330px-Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/440px-Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="458" data-file-height="570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gianfrancesco_Poggio_Bracciolini_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poggio Bracciolini (1380–1459), an early Renaissance humanist, book collector, and reformer of script, who served as papal secretary<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Contrary to a still widely held interpretation that originated in Voigt's celebrated contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and which was adopted wholeheartedly – especially by modern thinkers calling themselves "humanists" –<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> most specialists today do not characterise Renaissance humanism as a philosophical movement, nor in any way as anti-Christian or even anti-clerical. A modern historian has this to say:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Humanism was not an ideological programme but a body of literary knowledge and linguistic skill based on the "revival of good letters", which was a revival of a late-antique philology and grammar, This is how the word "humanist" was understood by contemporaries, and if scholars would agree to accept the word in this sense rather than in the sense in which it was used in the nineteenth century we might be spared a good deal of useless argument. That humanism had profound social and even political consequences of the life of Italian courts is not to be doubted. But the idea that as a movement it was in some way inimical to the Church, or to the conservative social order in general is one that has been put forward for a century and more without any substantial proof being offered.</p><div class="poem">
</div><p>The nineteenth-century historian Jacob Burckhardt, in his classic work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Civilization_of_the_Renaissance_in_Italy" title="The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy">The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy</a></i>, noted as a "curious fact" that some men of the new culture were "men of the strictest piety or even ascetics". If he had meditated more deeply on the meaning of the careers of such humanists as Abrogio Traversari (1386–1439), the General of the Camaldolese Order, perhaps he would not have gone on to describe humanism in unqualified terms like "pagan", and thus helped precipitate a century of infertile debate about the possible existence of something called "Christian humanism" which ought to be opposed to "pagan humanism".</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Peter Partner, <i>Renaissance Rome, Portrait of a Society 1500–1559</i> (University of California Press 1979) pp. 14–15.</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The <i>umanisti</i> criticized what they considered the barbarous Latin of the universities, but the revival of the humanities largely did not conflict with the teaching of traditional university subjects, which went on as before.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nor did the humanists view themselves as in conflict with Christianity. Some, like Salutati, were the Chancellors of Italian cities, but the majority (including Petrarch) were ordained as priests, and many worked as senior officials of the Papal court. Humanist Renaissance popes <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">Nicholas V</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_II" title="Pope Pius II">Pius II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_IV" title="Pope Sixtus IV">Sixtus IV</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Leo X</a> wrote books and amassed huge libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a>, in fact, there was a hope that more direct knowledge of the wisdom of antiquity, including the writings of the Church fathers, the earliest known Greek texts of the Christian Gospels, and in some cases even the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>, would initiate a harmonious new era of universal agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> With this end in view, Renaissance Church authorities afforded humanists what in retrospect appears a remarkable degree of freedom of thought.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> One humanist, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a> Platonist <a href="/wiki/Gemistus_Pletho" title="Gemistus Pletho">Gemistus Pletho</a> (1355–1452), based in <a href="/wiki/Mystras" title="Mystras">Mystras</a>, Greece (but in contact with humanists in Florence, Venice, and Rome) taught a Christianised version of pagan <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_to_the_sources">Back to the sources</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG/220px-Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG/330px-Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG/440px-Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG 2x" data-file-width="806" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quentin_Massys-_Erasmus_of_Rotterdam.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_of_Rotterdam" class="mw-redirect" title="Erasmus of Rotterdam">Erasmus of Rotterdam</a></div></div></div>
<p>The humanists' close study of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> literary texts soon enabled them to discern historical differences in the writing styles of different periods. By analogy with what they saw as decline of Latin, they applied the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_fontes" title="Ad fontes">ad fontes</a></i>, or back to the sources, across broad areas of learning, seeking out manuscripts of <a href="/wiki/Patristic" class="mw-redirect" title="Patristic">Patristic</a> literature as well as pagan authors. In 1439, while employed in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> at the court of <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_V_of_Aragon" title="Alfonso V of Aragon">Alfonso V of Aragon</a> (at the time engaged in a dispute with the Papal States) the humanist <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a> used stylistic textual analysis, now called <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>, to prove that the <a href="/wiki/Donation_of_Constantine" title="Donation of Constantine">Donation of Constantine</a>, which purported to confer temporal powers on the Pope of Rome, was an 8th-century forgery.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> For the next 70 years, however, neither Valla nor any of his contemporaries thought to apply the techniques of philology to other controversial manuscripts in this way. Instead, after the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> to the Turks in 1453, which brought a flood of Greek Orthodox refugees to Italy, humanist scholars increasingly turned to the study of <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermeticism</a>, hoping to bridge the differences between the Greek and Roman Churches, and even between Christianity itself and the non-Christian world.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The refugees brought with them Greek manuscripts, not only of Plato and Aristotle, but also of the Christian Gospels, previously unavailable in the Latin West.
</p><p>After 1517, when the new invention of printing made these texts widely available, the Dutch humanist <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, who had studied Greek at the Venetian printing house of <a href="/wiki/Aldus_Manutius" title="Aldus Manutius">Aldus Manutius</a>, began a philological analysis of the Gospels in the spirit of Valla, comparing the Greek originals with their Latin translations with a view to correcting errors and discrepancies in the latter. Erasmus, along with the French humanist <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Lef%C3%A8vre_d%27%C3%89taples" title="Jacques Lefèvre d&#39;Étaples">Jacques Lefèvre d'Étaples</a>, began issuing new translations, laying the groundwork for the Protestant Reformation. Henceforth Renaissance humanism, particularly in the German North, became concerned with religion, while Italian and French humanism concentrated increasingly on scholarship and philology addressed to a narrow audience of specialists, studiously avoiding topics that might offend despotic rulers or which might be seen as corrosive of faith. After the Reformation, critical examination of the Bible did not resume until the advent of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Higher_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher criticism">Higher criticism</a> of the 19th-century German <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCbingen_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Tübingen school">Tübingen school</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span></h4>
<p>The <i>ad fontes</i> principle also had many applications. The re-discovery of ancient manuscripts brought a more profound and accurate knowledge of ancient philosophical schools such as <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, whose Pagan wisdom the humanists, like the Church fathers of old, tended, at least initially, to consider as deriving from divine revelation and thus adaptable to a life of Christian virtue.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The line from a drama of <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a>, <i>Homo sum, humani nihil a me alienum puto</i> (or with <i>nil</i> for <i>nihil</i>), meaning "I am a human being, I think nothing human alien to me",<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> known since antiquity through the endorsement of Saint Augustine, gained renewed currency as epitomising the humanist attitude. The statement, in a play modeled or borrowed from a (now lost) Greek comedy by Menander, may have originated in a lighthearted vein&#160;&#8211;&#160;as a comic rationale for an old man's meddling&#160;&#8211;&#160;but it quickly became a proverb and throughout the ages was quoted with a deeper meaning, by Cicero and Saint Augustine, to name a few, and most notably by <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a>. Richard Bauman writes:
</p>
<blockquote><p><i>Homo sum: humani nihil a me alienum puto.</i>, I am a human being: and I deem nothing pertaining to humanity is foreign to me.</p></blockquote>
<p>The words of the comic playwright P.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>Terentius Afer reverberated across the Roman world of the mid-2nd century BCE and beyond. Terence, an African and a former slave, was well placed to preach the message of universalism, of the essential unity of the human race, that had come down in philosophical form from the Greeks, but needed the pragmatic muscles of Rome in order to become a practical reality. The influence of Terence's felicitous phrase on Roman thinking about human rights can hardly be overestimated. Two hundred years later Seneca ended his seminal exposition of the unity of humankind with a clarion-call:
</p>
<blockquote><p>There is one short rule that should regulate human relationships. All that you see, both divine and human, is one. We are parts of the same great body. Nature created us from the same source and to the same end. She imbued us with mutual affection and sociability, she taught us to be fair and just, to suffer injury rather than to inflict it. She bid us extend our hands to all in need of help. Let that well-known line be in our heart and on our lips: <i>Homo sum, humani nihil a me alienum puto</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Better acquaintance with Greek and Roman technical writings also influenced the development of European science (see the <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_the_Renaissance" title="History of science in the Renaissance">history of science in the Renaissance</a>). This was despite what <a href="/wiki/A._C._Crombie" class="mw-redirect" title="A. C. Crombie">A. C. Crombie</a> (viewing the Renaissance in the 19th-century manner as a chapter in the heroic March of Progress) calls "a backwards-looking admiration for antiquity", in which Platonism stood in opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> concentration on the observable properties of the physical world.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> But Renaissance humanists, who considered themselves as restoring the glory and nobility of antiquity, had no interest in scientific innovation. However, by the mid-to-late 16th century, even the universities, though still dominated by Scholasticism, began to demand that Aristotle be read in accurate texts edited according to the principles of Renaissance philology, thus setting the stage for Galileo's quarrels with the outmoded habits of Scholasticism.
</p><p>Just as artist and inventor <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;partaking of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">zeitgeist</a></i> though not himself a humanist&#160;&#8211;&#160;advocated study of human <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, nature, and weather to enrich Renaissance works of art, so Spanish-born humanist <a href="/wiki/Juan_Luis_Vives" title="Juan Luis Vives">Juan Luis Vives</a> (c. 1493–1540) advocated observation, craft, and practical techniques to improve the formal teaching of Aristotelian philosophy at the universities, helping to free them from the grip of Medieval Scholasticism.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Thus, the stage was set for the adoption of an approach to <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirical</a> observations and experimentation of the physical universe, making possible the advent of the age of scientific inquiry that followed the Renaissance.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was in education that the humanists' program had the most lasting results, their curriculum and methods:
</p>
<blockquote><p>were followed everywhere, serving as models for the Protestant Reformers as well as the Jesuits. The humanistic school, animated by the idea that the study of classical languages and literature provided valuable information and intellectual discipline as well as moral standards and a civilised taste for future rulers, leaders, and professionals of its society, flourished without interruption, through many significant changes, until our own century, surviving many religious, political and social revolutions. It has but recently been replaced, though not yet completely, by other more practical and less demanding forms of education.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Renaissance_to_modern_humanism">From Renaissance to modern humanism</span></h3>
<p>Renaissance scholars associated with humanism were religious, but inveighed against the abuses of the Church, if not against the Church itself.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> For them, the word <a href="/wiki/Secularism#Overview" title="Secularism">"secular"</a> carried no connotations of disbelief&#160;&#8211;&#160;that would come later, in the nineteenth century. In the Renaissance to be secular meant simply to be in the world rather than in a monastery. Petrarch frequently admitted that his brother Gherardo's life as a Carthusian monk was superior to his own (although Petrarch himself was in <a href="/wiki/Minor_Orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor Orders">Minor Orders</a> and was employed by the Church all his life). He hoped that he could do some good by winning earthly glory and praising virtue, inferior though that might be to a life devoted solely to prayer. By embracing a non-theistic philosophic base,<sup id="cite_ref-How_Should_We_Then_Live_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Should_We_Then_Live-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> however, the methods of the humanists, combined with their eloquence, would ultimately have a corrosive effect on established authority.
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Yet it was from the Renaissance that modern Secular Humanism grew, with the development of an important split between reason and religion. This occurred as the church's complacent authority was exposed in two vital areas. In science, Galileo's support of the Copernican revolution upset the church's adherence to the theories of Aristotle, exposing them as false. In theology, the Dutch scholar Erasmus with his new Greek text showed that the Roman Catholic adherence to Jerome's Vulgate was frequently in error. A tiny wedge was thus forced between reason and authority, as both of them were then understood.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>For some, this meant turning back to the Bible as the source of authority instead of the Catholic Church, for others it was a split from theism altogether. This was the main divisive line between the Reformation and the Renaissance,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> which dealt with the same basic problems, supported the same science based on reason and empirical research, but had a different set of presuppositions (theistic versus naturalistic).<sup id="cite_ref-How_Should_We_Then_Live_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Should_We_Then_Live-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nineteenth_and_twentieth_centuries">Nineteenth and twentieth centuries</span></h3><p>
The phrase the "religion of humanity" is sometimes attributed to American <a href="/wiki/Founding_Father" class="mw-redirect" title="Founding Father">Founding Father</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>, though as yet unattested in his surviving writings. According to Tony Davies: </p><blockquote><p>Paine called himself a <i>theophilanthropist</i>, a word combining the Greek for "God", "love", and "humanity", and indicating that while he believed in the existence of a creating intelligence in the universe, he entirely rejected the claims made by and for all existing religious doctrines, especially their miraculous, transcendental and salvationist pretensions. The Parisian "Society of Theophilanthropy" which he sponsored, is described by his biographer as "a forerunner of the ethical and humanist societies that proliferated later" ... [Paine's book] the trenchantly witty <i>Age of Reason</i> (1793) ... pours scorn on the supernatural pretensions of scripture, combining Voltairean mockery with Paine's own style of taproom ridicule to expose the absurdity of a theology built on a collection of incoherent Levantine folktales.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Davies identifies Paine's <i> <a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i> as "the link between the two major narratives of what <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Lyotard" title="Jean-François Lyotard">Jean-François Lyotard</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> calls the narrative of legitimation": the rationalism of the 18th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">Philosophes</a></i> and the radical, historically based German 19th-century Biblical criticism of the <a href="/wiki/Hegelian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hegelian">Hegelians</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Friedrich_Strauss" class="mw-redirect" title="David Friedrich Strauss">David Friedrich Strauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>. "The first is political, largely French in inspiration, and projects 'humanity as the hero of liberty'. The second is philosophical, German, seeks the totality and autonomy of knowledge, and stresses <i>understanding</i> rather than freedom as the key to human fulfilment and emancipation. The two themes converged and competed in complex ways in the 19th century and beyond, and between them set the boundaries of its various humanisms.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <i>Homo homini deus est</i> ("The human being is a god to humanity" or "god is nothing [other than] the human being to himself"), Feuerbach had written.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victorian novelist Mary Ann Evans, known to the world as <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>, translated Strauss's <i>Das Leben Jesu</i> (<i>"The Life of Jesus"</i>, 1846) and Ludwig Feuerbach's <i>Das Wesen Christianismus</i> ("The Essence of Christianity"). She wrote to a friend:
</p>
<blockquote><p>the fellowship between man and man which has been the principle of development, social and moral, is not dependent on conceptions of what is not man ... the idea of God, so far as it has been a high spiritual influence, is the ideal of goodness entirely human (i.e., an exaltation of the human).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Eliot and her circle, who included her companion <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Lewes" title="George Henry Lewes">George Henry Lewes</a> (the biographer of <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abolitionism in the United Kingdom">abolitionist</a> and social theorist <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Martineau" title="Harriet Martineau">Harriet Martineau</a>, were much influenced by the positivism of <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Auguste Comte</a>, whom Martineau had translated. Comte had proposed an atheistic <i>culte</i> founded on human principles&#160;&#8211;&#160;a secular <a href="/wiki/Religion_of_Humanity" title="Religion of Humanity">Religion of Humanity</a> (which worshiped the dead, since most humans who have ever lived are dead), complete with holidays and liturgy, modeled on the rituals of what was seen as a discredited and dilapidated Catholicism.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Although Comte's English followers, like Eliot and Martineau, for the most part rejected the full gloomy panoply of his system, they liked the idea of a religion of humanity. Comte's austere vision of the universe, his injunction to "<i>vivre pour altrui</i>" ("live for others", from which comes the word "<a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruism</a>"),<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and his idealisation of women inform the works of Victorian novelists and poets from George Eliot and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a> to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a>.
</p><p>The British Humanistic Religious Association was formed as one of the earliest forerunners of contemporary chartered Humanist organisations in 1853 in London. This early group was democratically organised, with male and female members participating in the election of the leadership, and promoted knowledge of the sciences, philosophy, and the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1877, the word was used pejoratively, apparently for the first time in America, to describe <a href="/wiki/Felix_Adler_(Society_for_Ethical_Culture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Felix Adler (Society for Ethical Culture)">Felix Adler</a>. Adler, however, did not embrace the term, and instead coined the name "<a href="/wiki/Ethical_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethical Culture">Ethical Culture</a>" for his new movement &#160;&#8211;&#160;a movement which still exists in the now Humanist-affiliated New York Society for Ethical Culture.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, Ethical Culture Leaders wrote: "Today, the historic identification, Ethical Culture, and the modern description, Ethical Humanism, are used interchangeably."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Active in the early 1920s, <a href="/wiki/F.C.S._Schiller" class="mw-redirect" title="F.C.S. Schiller">F.C.S. Schiller</a> labelled his work "humanism" but for Schiller the term referred to the <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatist</a> philosophy he shared with <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>. In 1929, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Potter" title="Charles Francis Potter">Charles Francis Potter</a> founded the First Humanist Society of New York whose advisory board included <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>. Potter was a minister from the <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> tradition and in 1930 he and his wife, Clara Cook Potter, published <i>Humanism: A New Religion</i>. Throughout the 1930s, Potter was an advocate of such liberal causes as, <a href="/wiki/Women%E2%80%99s_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Women’s rights">women’s rights</a>, access to <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>, "civil divorce laws", and an end to capital punishment.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Raymond_B._Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="Raymond B. Bragg">Raymond B. Bragg</a>, the associate editor of <i>The New Humanist</i>, sought to consolidate the input of <a href="/wiki/Leon_Milton_Birkhead" title="Leon Milton Birkhead">Leon Milton Birkhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Potter" title="Charles Francis Potter">Charles Francis Potter</a>, and several members of the Western Unitarian Conference. Bragg asked <a href="/wiki/Roy_Wood_Sellars" title="Roy Wood Sellars">Roy Wood Sellars</a> to draft a document based on this information which resulted in the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Humanist_Manifesto" title="Humanist Manifesto">Humanist Manifesto</a></i> in 1933. Potter's book and the Manifesto became the cornerstones of modern humanism, the latter declaring a new religion by saying, "any religion that can hope to be a synthesising and dynamic force for today must be shaped for the needs of this age. To establish such a religion is a major necessity of the present." It then presented 15 theses of humanism as foundational principles for this new religion.
</p><p>In 1941, the <a href="/wiki/American_Humanist_Association" title="American Humanist Association">American Humanist Association</a> was organised. Noted members of The AHA included <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>, who was the president from 1985 until his death in 1992, and writer <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">Kurt Vonnegut</a>, who followed as honorary president until his death in 2007. <a href="/wiki/Gore_Vidal" title="Gore Vidal">Gore Vidal</a> became honorary president in 2009. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Buckman" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Buckman">Robert Buckman</a> was the head of the association in Canada, and is now an honorary president.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>After World War II, three prominent Humanists became the first directors of major divisions of the United Nations: <a href="/wiki/Julian_Huxley" title="Julian Huxley">Julian Huxley</a> of <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brock_Chisholm" title="Brock Chisholm">Brock Chisholm</a> of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Boyd-Orr" class="mw-redirect" title="John Boyd-Orr">John Boyd-Orr</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, <a href="/wiki/American_Humanist_Association" title="American Humanist Association">American Humanist Association</a>, along with other groups representing agnostics, atheists, and other freethinkers, joined to create the <a href="/wiki/Secular_Coalition_for_America" title="Secular Coalition for America">Secular Coalition for America</a> which advocates in Washington, D.C., for <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a> and nationally for the greater acceptance of nontheistic Americans. The Executive Director of Secular Coalition for America is Larry T. Decker.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_tradition">Scholarly tradition</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_humanists">Renaissance humanists</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a></div>
<p>"Renaissance humanism" is the name later given to a tradition of cultural and educational reform engaged in by civic and ecclesiastical chancellors, book collectors, educators, and writers, who by the late fifteenth century began to be referred to as <i>umanisti</i>—"humanists".<sup id="cite_ref-mann_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It developed during the fourteenth and the beginning of the fifteenth centuries, and was a response to the challenge of scholastic university education, which was then dominated by Aristotelian philosophy and logic. <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a> focused on preparing men to be doctors, lawyers or professional theologians, and was taught from approved textbooks in logic, natural philosophy, medicine, law and theology.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> There were important centres of humanism at <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">Mantua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferrara" title="Ferrara">Ferrara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urbino" title="Urbino">Urbino</a>.
</p><p>Humanists reacted against this utilitarian approach and the narrow pedantry associated with it. They sought to create a citizenry (frequently including women) able to speak and write with eloquence and clarity and thus capable of engaging the civic life of their communities and persuading others to virtuous and prudent actions. This was to be accomplished through the study of the <i><a href="/wiki/Humanitas" title="Humanitas">studia humanitatis</a></i>, today known as the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>: grammar, rhetoric, history, poetry and moral philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> As a program to revive the cultural—and particularly the literary—legacy and moral philosophy of classical antiquity, Humanism was a pervasive cultural mode and not the program of a few isolated geniuses like <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">Rabelais</a> or <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> as is still sometimes popularly believed.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-theistic_worldviews">Non-theistic worldviews</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_humanists">Secular humanists</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">Secular humanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:62px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Happyman.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Happyman.svg/60px-Happyman.svg.png" decoding="async" width="60" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Happyman.svg/90px-Happyman.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Happyman.svg/120px-Happyman.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="134" data-file-height="516" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Happyman.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Humanist "happy human" logo</div></div></div>
<p>Secular humanism is a comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a> or <a href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a> that embraces human <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_naturalism" title="Metaphysical naturalism">metaphysical naturalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distributive_justice" title="Distributive justice">distributive justice</a>, and consciously rejects <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> claims, <a href="/wiki/Ethical_monotheism" title="Ethical monotheism">theistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religiosity" title="Religiosity">religiosity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-americanhumanist_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-americanhumanist-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-humaniststudies_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-humaniststudies-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> It is sometimes referred to as Humanism (with a capital H and no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_modifier" title="Grammatical modifier">qualifying</a> adjective).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Humanist_and_Ethical_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="International Humanist and Ethical Union">International Humanist and Ethical Union</a> (<a href="/wiki/IHEU" class="mw-redirect" title="IHEU">IHEU</a>) is the world union of 117 Humanist, rationalist, <a href="/wiki/Irreligious" class="mw-redirect" title="Irreligious">irreligious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheistic">atheistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brights_movement" title="Brights movement">Bright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethical_movement" title="Ethical movement">Ethical Culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethought</a> organisations in 38 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Happy_Human" title="Happy Human">Happy Human</a>" is the official symbol of the IHEU as well as being regarded as a universally recognised symbol for secular humanism.
</p><p>According to the IHEU's bylaw 5.1:<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Humanism is a democratic and ethical life stance, which affirms that human beings have the <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural and legal rights">right</a> and responsibility to give meaning and shape to their own lives. It stands for the building of a more humane society through an ethic based on human and other natural values in the spirit of reason and free inquiry through human capabilities. It is not <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a>, and it does not accept <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> views of reality.</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_humanists">Religious humanists</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_humanism" title="Religious humanism">Religious humanism</a></div>
<p>"Religious humanists" are non-superstitious people who nevertheless see ethical humanism as their religion, and who seek to integrate (secular) humanist ethical philosophy with congregational <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a> centred on human needs, interests, and abilities. Though practitioners of religious humanism did not officially organise under the name of "humanism" until the late 19th and early 20th centuries, non-theistic religions paired with human-centred ethical philosophy have a long history. A unified <a href="/wiki/Ethical_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethical Culture">Ethical Culture</a> movement was first founded in 1876; its founder, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Adler_(Society_for_Ethical_Culture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Felix Adler (Society for Ethical Culture)">Felix Adler</a>, was a former member of the <a href="/wiki/Free_Religious_Association" title="Free Religious Association">Free Religious Association</a> and conceived of Ethical Culture as a new religion that would retain the ethical message at the heart of all religions. Ethical Culture was religious in the sense of playing a defining role in people's lives and addressing issues of ultimate concern. Nowadays religious humanists in the United States are represented by organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Ethical_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="American Ethical Union">American Ethical Union</a> and will simply describe themselves as "ethical humanists" or "humanists". Secular humanists and religious humanists organise together as part of larger national and international groupings, and differentiate themselves primarily in their attitude to the promotion of humanist thinking.
</p><p>Earlier attempts at inventing a secular religious tradition informed the Ethical Culture movement. The <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_Reason" title="Cult of Reason">Cult of Reason</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Culte de la Raison</i>) was a religion based on <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a> devised during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_H%C3%A9bert" title="Jacques Hébert">Jacques Hébert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gaspard_Chaumette" title="Pierre Gaspard Chaumette">Pierre Gaspard Chaumette</a> and their supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In 1793, the cathedral <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a> was turned into a "<a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Reason" title="Temple of Reason">Temple of Reason</a>" and for a time <a href="/wiki/Liberty_(goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberty (goddess)">Lady Liberty</a> replaced the Virgin Mary on several altars.<sup id="cite_ref-Herrick_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrick-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In the 1850s, <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Auguste Comte</a>, the Father of Sociology, founded <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">Positivism</a>, a "religion of humanity".<sup id="cite_ref-Humanism_as_the_Next_Step_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humanism_as_the_Next_Step-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> One of the earliest forerunners of contemporary chartered humanist organisations was the Humanistic Religious Association formed in 1853 in London. This early group was democratically organised, with male and female members participating in the election of the leadership and promoted knowledge of the sciences, philosophy, and the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-Humanism_as_the_Next_Step_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Humanism_as_the_Next_Step-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinction between so-called "ethical" humanists and "secular" humanists is most pronounced in the United States, although it is becoming less so over time. The philosophical distinction is not reflected at all in Canada, Latin America, Africa, or Asia, or most of Europe. In the UK, where the humanist movement was strongly influenced by Americans in the 19th century, the leading "ethical societies" and "ethical churches" evolved into secular humanist charities (e.g. the British Ethical Union became the British Humanist Association and later <a href="/wiki/Humanists_UK" title="Humanists UK">Humanists UK</a>).
