In Scandinavian countries, "<i lang="nn" title="Nynorsk language text">human-etik</i><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style:normal; display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">code: nno promoted to code: nn </span>" or [[:no:Humanetikk|"<i lang="nn" title="Nynorsk language text">humanetikk</i><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style:normal; display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">code: nno promoted to code: nn </span>"]] (roughly synonymous with ethical humanism) is a popular strand within humanism, originating from the works of Danish philosopher <a href="/wiki/Harald_H%C3%B8ffding" title="Harald Høffding">Harald Høffding</a>. The Norwegian Humanist Association, <a href="/wiki/Human-Etisk_Forbund" class="mw-redirect" title="Human-Etisk Forbund">Human-Etisk Forbund</a> (HEF, literally "Human-Ethical League"), belongs to this tendency. Over time, the emphasis on <i lang="nn" title="Nynorsk language text">human-etisk</i><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style:normal; display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">code: nno promoted to code: nn </span> has become less pronounced, and today HEF promotes both <i lang="nn" title="Nynorsk language text">humanisme</i><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style:normal; display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">code: nno promoted to code: nn </span> (secular humanism) and <i lang="nn" title="Nynorsk language text">human-etisk</i><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style:normal; display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">code: nno promoted to code: nn </span>. In Sweden, the main Swedish humanist group <a href="/wiki/Humanisterna" class="mw-redirect" title="Humanisterna">Humanisterna</a> ("Humanists") began as a "human-ethical association" like the Norwegian humanists before adopting the more prevalent secular humanist model popular in most of Europe. Today the distinction in Europe is mostly superficial.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<p>Polemics about humanism have sometimes assumed paradoxical twists and turns. Early-20th-century critics such as <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/T._E._Hulme" title="T. E. Hulme">T.&#160;E. Hulme</a>, and <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T.&#160;S. Eliot</a> considered humanism to be sentimental "slop" (Hulme)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or "an old bitch gone in the teeth" (Pound).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">Postmodern</a> critics who are self-described <a href="/wiki/Antihumanism" title="Antihumanism">anti-humanists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Lyotard" title="Jean-François Lyotard">Jean-François Lyotard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>, have asserted that humanism posits an overarching and excessively abstract notion of humanity or universal <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>, which can then be used as a pretext for imperialism and domination of those deemed somehow less than human. "Humanism fabricates the human as much as it fabricates the nonhuman animal", suggests Timothy Laurie, turning the human into what he calls "a placeholder for a range of attributes that have been considered most virtuous among humans (e.g. rationality, altruism), rather than most commonplace (e.g. hunger, anger)".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Kate_Soper" title="Kate Soper">Kate Soper</a><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> notes that by faulting humanism for falling short of its own benevolent ideals, anti-humanism thus frequently "secretes a humanist rhetoric".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his book, <i>Humanism</i> (1997), Tony Davies calls these critics "humanist anti-humanists". Critics of antihumanism, most notably <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a>, counter that while antihumanists may highlight humanism's failure to fulfill its emancipatory ideal, they do not offer an alternative emancipatory project of their own.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Others, like the German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Heidegger" class="mw-redirect" title="Heidegger">Heidegger</a>. considered themselves humanists on the model of the ancient Greeks but thought humanism applied only to the German "race" and specifically to the Nazis and thus, in Davies' words, were anti-humanist humanists.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Such a reading of Heidegger's thought is itself deeply controversial; Heidegger includes his own views and critique of Humanism in <a href="/wiki/Letter_on_Humanism" title="Letter on Humanism">Letter On Humanism</a>. Davies acknowledges that, after the horrific experiences of the wars of the 20th century, "it should no longer be possible to formulate phrases like 'the destiny of man' or the 'triumph of human reason' without an instant consciousness of the folly and brutality they drag behind them". For "it is almost impossible to think of a crime that has not been committed in the name of human reason". Yet, he continues, "it would be unwise to simply abandon the ground occupied by the historical humanisms. For one thing humanism remains on many occasions the only available alternative to bigotry and persecution. The freedom to speak and write, to organise and campaign in defence of individual or collective interests, to protest and disobey: all these can only be articulated in humanist terms."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern humanists, such as <a href="/wiki/Corliss_Lamont" title="Corliss Lamont">Corliss Lamont</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>, hold that humanity must seek for truth through reason and the best observable evidence and endorse <a href="/wiki/Scientific_skepticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific skepticism">scientific skepticism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>. However, they stipulate that decisions about right and wrong must be based on the individual and common good, with no consideration given to metaphysical or supernatural beings. The idea is to engage with what is human.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The ultimate goal is human flourishing; making life better for all humans, and as the most conscious species, also promoting concern for the welfare of other sentient beings and the planet as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  The focus is on doing good and living well in the here and now, and leaving the world a better place for those who come after. In 1925, the English mathematician and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> cautioned: "The prophecy of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> has now been fulfilled; and man, who at times dreamt of himself as a little lower than the angels, has submitted to become the servant and the minister of nature. It still remains to be seen whether the same actor can play both parts".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanistic_psychology">Humanistic psychology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">Humanistic psychology</a></div>
<p>Humanistic psychology is a psychological perspective that rose to prominence in the mid-20th century in response to <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>'s psychoanalytic theory and <a href="/wiki/B._F._Skinner" title="B. F. Skinner">B. F. Skinner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Behaviorism" title="Behaviorism">behaviorism</a>. The approach emphasizes an individual's inherent drive towards <a href="/wiki/Self-actualization" title="Self-actualization">self-actualization</a> and creativity.  Psychologists <a href="/wiki/Carl_Rogers" title="Carl Rogers">Carl Rogers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Maslow" title="Abraham Maslow">Abraham Maslow</a> introduced a positive, humanistic psychology in response to what they viewed as the overly pessimistic view of psychoanalysis in the early 1960s. Other sources include the philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">phenomenology</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Richard Bauman, <i>Human Rights in Ancient Rome</i> (Routledge Classical Monographs [1999]), pp. 74–75.</span>

<li id="cite_note-mann-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-mann_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-mann_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Mann, Nicholas (1996). <i>The Origins of Humanism</i>. Cambridge University Press. pp.&#160;1–2. <q>The term <i>umanista</i> was used, in fifteenth century Italian academic jargon to describe a teacher or student of classical literature including that of grammar and rhetoric. The English equivalent 'humanist' makes its appearance in the late sixteenth century with a similar meaning. Only in the nineteenth century, however, and probably for the first time in <a href="/wiki/Humanism_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Humanism in Germany">Germany</a> in 1809, is the attribute transformed into a substantive: <i>humanism</i>, standing for devotion to the literature of ancient Greece and Rome, and the humane values that may be derived from them.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Origins+of+Humanism&amp;rft.pages=1-2&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.aulast=Mann&amp;rft.aufirst=Nicholas&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-oxford-renaissance-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-oxford-renaissance_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Humanissime vir</i>, "most humane man", was the usual Latin way to address scholars. (Giustiniani, "Homo, Humanus, and the Meanings of Humanism"&#160;: 168.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There was a time when men wandered about in the manner of wild beasts. They conducted their affairs without the least guidance of reason but instead relied on bodily strength. There was no divine religion and the understanding of social duty was in no way cultivated. No one recognized the value inherent in an equitable code of law.(Cicero, <i>De Inventione</i>, I. I: 2, quoted in Quentin Skinner, <i>Visions of Politics, Volume 2:  Renaissance Virtues</i> [Cambridge University Press, 2002], p. 54.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A noted authority on the subject, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Oskar_Kristeller" title="Paul Oskar Kristeller">Paul Oskar Kristeller</a>, identified Renaissance humanism as a cultural and literary movement, which in its substance was not philosophical but which had important philosophical implications and consequences." "I have been unable to discover in the humanist literature any common philosophical doctrine," he wrote, "except a belief in the value of man and the humanities and in the revival of ancient learning." (Paul Oskar Kristeller, <i>Renaissance Thought: The Classic, Scholastic, and Humanist Strains</i> [New York, Harper and Row, 1961], p. 9). As the late Jacques Barzun has written:<blockquote><p>The path between the onset of the good letters and the modern humanist as freethinker or simply as scholar is circuitous but unbroken. If we look for what is common to the Humanists over the centuries we find two things: a body of accepted authors and a method of carrying on study and debate. The two go together with the belief that the best guides to the good life are <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">Reason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">Nature</a>. (Jacques Barzun, <i>From Dawn to Decadence :500 years of Western Cultural Life</i> [New York: HarperCollins, 2000], p. 45)</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.infidels.org/library/modern/edwin_wilson/manifesto/ch2.html">"Genesis of a Humanist Manifesto"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">14 May</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Genesis+of+a+Humanist+Manifesto&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.infidels.org%2Flibrary%2Fmodern%2Fedwin_wilson%2Fmanifesto%2Fch2.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111107221355/http://www.americanhumanist.org/who_we_are/about_humanism/Humanist_Manifesto_I">"Text of Humanist Manifesto I"</a>. Americanhumanist.org. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.americanhumanist.org/Who_We_Are/About_Humanism/Humanist_Manifesto_I">the original</a> on 7 November 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 November</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Text+of+Humanist+Manifesto+I&amp;rft.pub=Americanhumanist.org&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.americanhumanist.org%2FWho_We_Are%2FAbout_Humanism%2FHumanist_Manifesto_I&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although a distinction has often been drawn between secular and religious humanism, the <a href="/wiki/International_Humanist_and_Ethical_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="International Humanist and Ethical Union">International Humanist and Ethical Union</a> and similar organizations prefer to describe their life stance without qualification as 'Humanism'. See Nicolas Walter,  <i>Humanism: What's in the Word?</i> (London: RPA/BHA/Secular Society Ltd, 1937), p. 43.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Harold Blackham, Levi Fragell, Corliss Lamont, Harry Stopes-Roe,  Rob Tielman. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iheu.org/humanism-is-eight-letters-no-more">"Humanism is Eight Letters, No More"</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Humanism+is+Eight+Letters%2C+No+More&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iheu.org%2Fhumanism-is-eight-letters-no-more&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: uses authors parameter (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_uses_authors_parameter" title="Category:CS1 maint: uses authors parameter">link</a>)</span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Niethammer's book was entitled <i>Der Streit des Philanthropinismus und des Humanismus in der Theorie des Erziehungs-Unterrichts unsrer Zeit</i> (<i>The Dispute between Philanthropinism and Humanism in the Educational Theory of our Time</i>), which directly echoes Aulus Gellius's distinction between "philanthropy" and humane learning. Neithammer and other distinguished members of the movement they called "Neo-Humanism" (who included <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a>), felt that the curriculum imposed under Napoleon's occupation of Germany had been excessively oriented toward the practical and vocational. They wished to encourage individuals to practice life-long self cultivation and reflection, based on a study of the artistic, philosophical, and cultural masterpieces of (primarily) Greek civilization.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-See_1877-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-See_1877_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As J. A. Symonds remarked, "the word humanism has a German sound and is in fact modern" (See <i>The Renaissance in Italy</i> Vol. 2:71 n, 1877). Vito Giustiniani writes that in the German-speaking world "Humanist" while keeping its specific meaning (as scholar of Classical literature) "gave birth to further derivatives, such as <i>humanistisch</i> for those schools which later were to be called <i>humanistische Gymnasien</i>, with Latin and Greek as the main subjects of teaching (1784). Finally, <i>Humanismus</i> was introduced to denote 'classical education in general' (1808) and still later for the epoch and the achievements of the Italian humanists of the fifteenth century (1841). This is to say that 'humanism' for 'classical learning' appeared first in Germany, where it was once and for all sanctioned in this meaning by Georg Voigt (1859)". (Giustiniani, "Homo, Humanus, and the Meanings of Humanism"&#160;: 172.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>L'amour général de l'humanité ... vertu qui n'a point de nom parmi nous et que nous oserions appeler 'humanisme', puisqu'enfin il est temps de créer un mot pour une chose si belle et nécessaire</i>"; from the review <i>Ephémérides du citoyen ou Bibliothèque raisonée des sciences morales et politiques</i>, Chapter 16 (Dec, 17, 1765): 247, quoted in Giustiniani, "Homo, Humanus, and the Meanings of Humanism"&#160;: 175, note 38.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Rousseau himself devoutly believed in a personal God, his book, <i><a href="/wiki/Emile:_or,_On_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Emile: or, On Education">Emile: or, On Education</a></i>, does attempt to demonstrate that atheists can be virtuous. It was publicly burned. During the Revolution, <a href="/wiki/Jacobin_(politics)" title="Jacobin (politics)">Jacobins</a> instituted a cult of the Supreme Being along lines suggested by Rousseau. In the 19th-century French positivist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Auguste Comte</a> (1798–1857) founded a "religion of humanity", whose calendar and catechism echoed the former Revolutionary cult. See <a href="/wiki/Comtism" class="mw-redirect" title="Comtism">Comtism</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><i>The Oxford English Dictionary</i>. <b>VII</b> (2nd ed.). Oxford: Clarendon Press. 1989. pp.&#160;474–75.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+English+Dictionary&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=474-75&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Clarendon+Press&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>"Ma conviction intime est que la religion de l'avenir sera le pur</i> humanisme, <i>c’est-à-dire le culte de tout ce qui est de l'homme, la vie entière santifiée et éléve a une valeur moral"</i>. quoted in Giustiniani, "Homo, Humanus, and the Meanings of Humanism"&#160;: 175.</span>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The influence of Jacob Burckhardt's classic masterpiece of cultural history, <a href="/wiki/The_Civilisation_of_the_Renaissance_in_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Civilisation of the Renaissance in Italy">The Civilisation of the Renaissance in Italy</a> (1860) on subsequent Renaissance historiography is traced in Wallace K. Ferguson's <i>The Renaissance in Historical Thought: Five Centuries of Historical Interpretation</i> (1948).</span>

<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example the <i>Cambridge Dictionary of Philosophy,</i> adhering to the tenacious 19th-century narrative of the Renaissance as a complete break with the past established in 1860 by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Burckhardt" title="Jacob Burckhardt">Jacob Burckhardt</a>, describes the liberating effects of the re-discovery of classical writings this way:<blockquote><p>Here, one felt no weight of the supernatural pressing on the human mind, demanding homage and allegiance. Humanity—with all its distinct capabilities, talents, worries, problems, possibilities—was the centre of interest. It has been said that medieval thinkers philosophised on their knees, but, bolstered by the new studies, they dared to stand up and to rise to full stature.<cite class="citation encyclopaedia">"Humanism". <i>"The Cambridge Dictionary of Philosophy, Second Edition</i>. Cambridge University Press. 1999.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Humanism&amp;rft.btitle=%22The+Cambridge+Dictionary+of+Philosophy%2C+Second+Edition&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

"The term <i>umanista</i> was associated with the revival of the <i><a href="/wiki/Studia_humanitatis" class="mw-redirect" title="Studia humanitatis">studia humanitatis</a></i> "which included <i>grammatica</i>, <i>rhetorica</i>, poetics, <i>historia</i>, and <i>philosophia moralis</i>, as these terms were understood. Unlike the liberal arts of the eighteenth century, they did not include the visual arts, music, dancing or gardening. The humanities also failed to include the disciplines that were the chief subjects of instruction at the universities during the Later Middle Ages and throughout the Renaissance, such as theology, jurisprudence, and medicine, and the philosophical disciplines other than ethics, such as logic, natural philosophy, and metaphysics. In other words, humanism does not represent, as often believed, the sum total of Renaissance thought and learning, but only a well-defined sector of it. Humanism has its proper domain or home territory in the humanities, whereas all other areas of learning, including philosophy (apart from ethics), followed their own course, largely determined by their medieval tradition and by their steady transformation through new observations, problems, or theories. These disciplines were affected by humanism mainly from the outside and in an indirect way, though often quite strongly". (<a href="/wiki/Paul_Oskar_Kristeller" title="Paul Oskar Kristeller">Paul Oskar Kristeller</a>, <i>Humanism</i>, pp. 113–14, in Charles B. Schmitt, <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Skinner" title="Quentin Skinner">Quentin Skinner</a> (editors), <i>The Cambridge History of Renaissance Philosophy</i> [1990].</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See their respective entries in Sir John Hale's <i>Concise Encyclopaedia of the Italian Renaissance</i> (Oxford University Press, 1981).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To later generations, the Dutch humanist, <a href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Desiderius Erasmus</a>, epitomised this reconciling tendency). According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>, "<a href="/wiki/European_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="European Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinkers remembered Erasmus (not quite accurately) as a precursor of modern intellectual freedom and a foe of both Protestant and Catholic dogmatism". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/erasmus/.">Erasmus himself was not much interested in the Kabbalah, but several other humanists were, notably Pico della Mirandola</a>. See <a href="/wiki/Christian_Kabbalah" title="Christian Kabbalah">Christian Kabbalah</a>.)</span>

<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bergin, Thomas; Speake, Jennifer (1987). <i>The Encyclopedia of the Renaissance</i>. Oxford: Facts On File Publications. pp.&#160;216–17.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+the+Renaissance&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=216-17&amp;rft.pub=Facts+On+File+Publications&amp;rft.date=1987&amp;rft.aulast=Bergin&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft.au=Speake%2C+Jennifer&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Only thirteen of Pico della Mirandola's nine hundred theses were thought theologically objectionable by the papal commission that examined them.... [This] suggests that, in spite of his publicly expressed contempt in his <i>Apologia</i> for their intellectual inadequacies, the Curial authorities hardly saw these theses as the work of a dangerous theological modernist like Luther or Calvin. Unorthodox though they were, most of the issues raised in them had been the subject of theological dispute for centuries and the commission ... condemned him not for innovations but for 'reviving several of the errors of gentile philosophers which are already disproved and obsolete'". Davies (1997), p 103.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Popkin" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard H. Popkin">Richard H. Popkin</a> (editor), <i>The Columbia History of Western Philosophy</i> (1998), pp. 293, 301.</span>

<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">More than 100 years earlier, Dante in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a> (c. 1308–1321) had pinpointed the Donation of Constantine (which he accepted as genuine) as a great mistake and the cause of all the political and religious problems of Italy, including the corruption of the Church. Although Dante had thunderously attacked the idea that the Church could have temporal as well as spiritual powers, it remained to Valla to conclusively prove that the legal justification for such powers was spurious.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ironically, it was a humanist scholar, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Casaubon" title="Isaac Casaubon">Isaac Casaubon</a>, in the 17th century, who would use philology to show that the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Hermeticum" class="mw-redirect" title="Corpus Hermeticum">Corpus Hermeticum</a> was not of great antiquity, as had been asserted in the 4th century by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Augustine">Saint Augustine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>, but dated from the Christian era. See Anthony Grafton, <i>Defenders of the Text: The Traditions of Scholarship in an Age of Science, 1450–1800</i> (Harvard University Press, 1991).</span>

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation encyclopaedia">"Humanism". <i>Encyclopedic Dictionary of Religion</i>. <b>F–N</b>. <li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Homo</i> in Latin specifically means "human being", in contrast to <i>vir</i>, "man", and <i>mulier</i>, "woman": Annabel Robinson, <i>The Life and Work of Jane Ellen Harrison</i> (Oxford University Press, 2002), p. 206; Tore Janson, <i>A Natural History of Latin</i> (Oxford University Press, 2004), p. 281; Timothy J. Moore, <i>Roman Theatre</i> (Cambridge University Press, 2012), p. 62 (note to the line in Terence); as a "watchword" for humanists, <i>Humanism and the Humanities in the Twenty-First Century</i>, edited by William S. Haney and Peter Malekin (Associated University Presses, 2001), p. 171; similar <i>homo sum</i> declaration by Seneca, James Ker, <i>The Deaths of Seneca</i> (Oxford University Press, 2009), p. 193.</span>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davies, <i>Humanism</i>, p. 28.</span>

<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">quoted in Davies (1997), p. 27.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Comte's secular religion is no vague effusion of humanistic piety, but a complete system of belief and ritual, with liturgy and sacraments, priesthood and pontiff, all organised around the public veneration of Humanity, the <i>Nouveau Grand-Être Suprême</i> (New Supreme Great Being), later to be supplemented in a positivist trinity by the <i>Grand Fétish</i> (the Earth) and the <i>Grand Milieu</i> (Destiny)". According to Davies (pp. 28–29), Comte's austere and "slightly dispiriting" philosophy of humanity viewed as alone in an indifferent universe (which can only be explained by "positive" science) and with nowhere to turn but to each other, was even more influential in Victorian England than the theories of Charles Darwin or Karl Marx.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davies, p. 29.</span>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><blockquote><p>Early Italian humanism, which in many respects continued the grammatical and rhetorical traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, not merely provided the old <a href="/wiki/Trivium" title="Trivium">Trivium</a> with a new and more ambitious name (<i>Studia humanitatis</i>), but also increased its actual scope, content and significance in the curriculum of the schools and universities and in its own extensive literary production. The <i>studia humanitatis</i> excluded logic, but they added to the traditional grammar and rhetoric not only history, Greek, and moral philosophy, but also made poetry, once a sequel of grammar and rhetoric, the most important member of the whole group. (Paul Oskar Kristeller, <i>Renaissance Thought II: Papers on Humanism and the Arts</i> [New York: Harper Torchbooks, 1965], p. 178.)</p></blockquote> See also Kristeller's <i>Renaissance Thought I</i>, "Humanism and Scholasticism In the Italian Renaissance", <i>Byzantion</i> 17 (1944–45):  346–74. Reprinted in <i>Renaissance Thought</i> (New York: Harper Torchbooks), 1961.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vito Giustiniani gives as an example of an out-dated, but still pervasive view, that of Corliss Lamont, who described Renaissance Humanism as, "first and foremost a revolt against the otherworldliness of mediaeval Christianity, a turning away from preoccupation with personal immortality to make the best of life in this world. Renaissance writers like Rabelais and Erasmus gave eloquent voice to this new joy of living and to the sheer exuberance of existence. For the Renaissance the ideal human being was no longer the ascetic monk, but a new type – the universal man the many-sided personality delighting in every kind of this-earthly achievements. The great Italian artists, Leonardo da Vinci and Michelangelo, typified this ideal." (Giustiniani, "Homo, Humanus, and the Meanings of Humanism": 192.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-americanhumanist-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-americanhumanist_67-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Edwords, Fred (1989). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100130233229/http://www.americanhumanist.org/who_we_are/about_humanism/What_is_Humanism">"What Is Humanism?"</a>. American Humanist Association. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.americanhumanist.org/who_we_are/about_humanism/What_is_Humanism">the original</a> on 30 January 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 August</span> 2009</span>. <q>Secular Humanism is an outgrowth of eighteenth century enlightenment rationalism and nineteenth century freethought... Secular and Religious Humanists both share the same worldview and the same basic principles... From the standpoint of philosophy alone, there is no difference between the two. It is only in the definition of religion and in the practice of the philosophy that Religious and Secular Humanists effectively disagree.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=What+Is+Humanism%3F&amp;rft.pub=American+Humanist+Association&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft.aulast=Edwords&amp;rft.aufirst=Fred&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.americanhumanist.org%2Fwho_we_are%2Fabout_humanism%2FWhat_is_Humanism&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> A decidedly anti-theistic version of secular humanism, however, is developed by Adolf Grünbaum, 'In Defense of Secular Humanism' (1995), in his <i>Collected Works</i> (edited by Thomas Kupka), vol. I, New York: Oxford University Press 2013, ch. 6 (pp. 115–48)</span>

<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tony Davies, <i>Humanism</i> (Routledge, 1997) p. 48.</span>

<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFLaurie2015" class="citation">Laurie, Timothy (2015), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/10912960">"Becoming-Animal Is A Trap For Humans"</a>, <i>Deleuze and the Non-Human</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Deleuze+and+the+Non-Human&amp;rft.atitle=Becoming-Animal+Is+A+Trap+For+Humans&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.aulast=Laurie&amp;rft.aufirst=Timothy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.academia.edu%2F10912960&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> eds. Hannah Stark and Jon Roffe.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">in <i>Humanism and Anti-humanism (Problems of Modern European Thought)</i> (La Salle, Illinois: Open Court Press, 1986, p. 128.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">quoted in Davies (1997) p. 49.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Habermas accepts some criticisms leveled at traditional humanism but believes that humanism must be rethought and revised rather than simply abandoned.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The antihhumanist Humanism of Heidegger and the humanist antihumanism of Foucault and Althusser" (Davies [1997]), p. 131.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davies (1997), pp. 131–32</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Conscience, the sense of right and wrong and the insistent call of one's better, more idealistic, more social-minded self, is a social product. Feelings of right and wrong that at first have their locus within the family gradually develop into a pattern for the tribe or city, then spread to the larger unit of the nation, and finally from the nation to humanity as a whole. Humanism sees no need for resorting to supernatural explanations, or sanctions at any point in the ethical process" (<cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Corliss_Lamont" title="Corliss Lamont">Lamont, Corliss</a> (1997). <i>The Philosophy of Humanism, Eighth Edition</i>. Humanist Press: Amherst, New York. pp.&#160;252–53. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-931779-07-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-931779-07-3"><bdi>0-931779-07-3</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Philosophy+of+Humanism%2C+Eighth+Edition&amp;rft.pages=252-53&amp;rft.pub=Humanist+Press%3A+Amherst%2C+New+York&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=0-931779-07-3&amp;rft.aulast=Lamont&amp;rft.aufirst=Corliss&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See for example <cite class="citation book">Kurtz, Paul (2000). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/humanistmanifest00kurt_0"><i>Humanist manifesto 2000&#160;: a call for a new planetary humanism</i></a>. Amherst, NY: Prometheus Books. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/157392783X" title="Special:BookSources/157392783X"><bdi>157392783X</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Humanist+manifesto+2000+%3A+a+call+for+a+new+planetary+humanism&amp;rft.place=Amherst%2C+NY&amp;rft.pub=Prometheus+Books&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.isbn=157392783X&amp;rft.aulast=Kurtz&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fhumanistmanifest00kurt_0&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHumanism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Science and the Modern World</i> (New York: Simon and Schuster, [1925] 1997) p. 96.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
